10.1016/j.jmp.2012.01.003
Lexicographic semiorders are mathematical structures that provide the foundation for many models of decision making under risk ( Fishburn, 1991; Nakamura, 1997; Tversky, 1969 ) including recent models such as the Priority Heuristic 
                         1 
                      
                      
                         1 
                         The priority heuristic can be characterized as either a lexicographic semiorder or a lexicographic interval order depending on the experimental stimuli the theory is being applied to. 
                       ( Brandstätter, Gigerenzer, & Hertwig, 2006 ) and  Manzini and Mariotti’s (2012)  theory of choice by lexicographic semiorders. In particular, lexicographic semiorders have been used extensively to model and test the well-known transitivity of preference axiom 
                         2 
                      
                      
                         2 
                         A decision maker (DM) is said to be  transitive  in his or her preferences, if, and only if, for any three choice alternatives  
                               
                                  ( 
                                  a 
                                  , 
                                  b 
                                  , 
                                  c 
                                  ) 
                               
                            , if the DM prefers  
                               a 
                             to  
                               b 
                            , and prefers  
                               b 
                             to  
                               c 
                            , then the DM must prefer  
                               a 
                             to  
                               c 
                            . 
                       ( Birnbaum, 2008, 2010; Birnbaum & Gutierrez, 2007; Birnbaum & LaCroix, 2008; Lopes, 1995; Montgomery, 1977; Ranyard, 1977, 1982; Regenwetter, Dana, & Davis-Stober, 2011; Roelofsma & Read, 2000; Tversky, 1969 ).

A  lexicographic semiorder  is a binary relation on  
                         A 
                       that can be characterized by an ordered collection of semiorders; for an overview of lexicographic semiorders see  Pirlot and Vincke (1997) .  Simple lexicographic semiorders  are a special case of lexicographic semiorders that can be characterized by two semiorders whose traces define weak orderings which are the reverse of one another ( Davis-Stober, 2010 ).

                      . Property (i) in the above definition is useful for modeling decision environments where the attributes of a set of choice alternatives “trade-off” with one another. For example, consider a set of distinct consumer products with two basic attributes: price and quality. Linearly ordering these products by their price will typically be the reverse of ordering these same products by their quality. Within decision making under risk, lotteries are often selected such that the probability of winning a prize “trades-off” with the prize amount (e.g.,  Montgomery, 1977 ;  Ranyard, 1977, 1982 ;  Roelofsma & Read, 2000  and  Tversky, 1969 ).

In this article, I consider the set of all simple lexicographic semiorders compatible with a fixed linear ordering of  
                         A 
                      , termed  compatible simple lexicographic semiorders . I then define the  compatible simple lexicographic semiorder polytope , denoted  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                      , as the convex hull of the set of characteristic vectors of all simple lexicographic semiorders compatible with an arbitrary, fixed linear ordering of  
                         A 
                      . In other words, I consider a high-dimensional polyhedron whose “extremal points” are the set of compatible simple lexicographic semiorders; see  Ziegler (1995)  for an introduction to convex polytopes. I demonstrate that the polytope,  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                      , provides a representation of the set of all probability distributions over the set of compatible simple lexicographic semiorders. I present seven classes of linear inequalities that provide a complete linear description of  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       for any finite  
                         n 
                      , where  
                         
                            | 
                            A 
                            | 
                         
                         = 
                         n 
                      . I go on to prove that this is a minimal such list, i.e., it is  facet-defining . I also enumerate the number of facets and the number of vertices of  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       as a function of  
                         n 
                      .

The polytope,  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                      , differs from other similarly motivated 0/1 polytopes in that it is defined via a set of relations compatible with a  fixed  linear ordering of  
                         A 
                      . There are two major reasons for considering this restricted definition. First, when considering theories based upon lexicographic semiorders, there are often ‘natural’ orderings on the choice alternatives induced by their attributes. For example, in decision-making under risk, simple 2-branch lotteries are comprised of two attributes: the probability of winning a monetary prize and the monetary value of that prize. A standard lexicographic semiorder theory would posit that decision makers (DMs) compare the lotteries by their attributes in a pairwise, sequential fashion. Given the modest assumptions that DMs desire more money to less money and, likewise, they prefer a higher probability of winning a prize to a lower probability, we can fix the underlying ordering of the choice alternatives according to these natural attribute orderings. For example, if we fix the linear ordering of these lotteries according to the probability of winning attribute then the vertices of  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       correspond to the set of all simple lexicographic semiorders such that the probability of winning attribute is considered first ( 
                         
                            
                               S 
                            
                            
                               1 
                            
                         
                      ) followed by payoff ( 
                         
                            
                               S 
                            
                            
                               2 
                            
                         
                      ).

Second, many decision theories in general, and those based upon lexicographic semiorders in particular, are  deterministic , in that they are not defined in terms of random variables and cannot explicitly account for probabilistic data. Many researchers have noted that this presents non-trivial conceptual and statistical problems when empirically evaluating such theories (e.g.,  Hey, 2005 ;  Luce & Narens, 1994 ). The polytope,  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                      , can be a powerful tool to help us evaluate the descriptive accuracy of theories based upon lexicographic semiorders by providing a theoretic account of choice variability while at the same time conforming to the decision making process under investigation. I demonstrate how the polytope,  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                      , can be considered as a probabilistic mixture model over the set of compatible simple lexicographic semiorders. Such a mixture model has a substantive interpretation; whether by fatigue or ‘change of mind’, a DM’s thresholds (and/or utility functions under monotonicity constraints) may vary, but at  all  times the DM’s decision making process follows a simple lexicographic semiorder  structure . At face value, such a model appears quite permissive, in that it could accommodate many possible datasets. On the contrary, I demonstrate that the polytope  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                      , when interpreted as a mixture model, greatly constrains the set of permissible ternary choice probabilities.

This paper is organized as follows. First, I present some preliminary definitions concerning convex polytopes and define a matrix representation of compatible simple lexicographic semiorders. Second, I prove a series of results concerning the facet structure of  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                      , including providing a complete list of facet-defining inequalities for any finite  
                         n 
                      . Third, I demonstrate that  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       can be equivalently characterized as a mixture model of probabilistic choice. I demonstrate that the facets of  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                      , when interpreted as constraints on ternary choice probabilities, are both necessary and sufficient to represent a decision maker’s preferences as a sampling process over compatible simple lexicographic semiorders. I also illustrate how choice data can be analyzed under  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       using existing statistical methods. I conclude with a summary of these results and consider future directions and open problems.

Let  
                         X 
                       be a collection of points in  
                         
                            
                               R 
                            
                            
                               m 
                            
                         
                      , where  
                         m 
                         ≥ 
                         0 
                      . The  convex hull  of  
                         X 
                       is defined as the minimal convex set containing  
                         X 
                      . Equivalently, the convex hull of  
                         X 
                       can be described as the set of all convex combinations of a finite number of points from  
                         X 
                      . A  convex polytope  is defined either as the convex hull of a finite number of points in  
                         
                            
                               R 
                            
                            
                               m 
                            
                         
                       ( 
                         V 
                      -representation) or, equivalently, as a bounded intersection of finitely many closed half-spaces, each of which can be defined by an affine inequality ( 
                         H 
                      -representation) ( Ziegler, 1995 ). Thus, if  
                         X 
                       were a  finite  collection of points in  
                         
                            
                               R 
                            
                            
                               m 
                            
                         
                       the convex hull of  
                         X 
                       would be a convex polytope, equivalently defined by either the set of all convex combinations of points from  
                         X 
                       or as the solution space to a system of linear inequalities. The  dimension  of a convex polytope is defined as the dimension of its affine hull ( Ziegler, 1995 ). A polytope is  full dimensional  in  
                         
                            
                               R 
                            
                            
                               m 
                            
                         
                       if and only if its dimension is  
                         m 
                      . A  face  of a convex polytope is defined as the intersection of the convex polytope with a hyperplane, such that the convex polytope is entirely contained in one of the two half-spaces defined by that hyperplane. A proper face of maximal dimension is called a  facet . A collection of linear inequalities is  facet-defining  for a full dimensional convex polytope if, and only if, its solution space is the convex polytope in question and if it is a  minimal  such list, i.e., no inequalities are implied by any combination of the other inequalities.

                       These inequalities are a refinement from those listed in  Davis-Stober (2010)  in that, having removed the main diagonal, we no longer require that all diagonal entries are equal to zero. This list also removes any redundancies from the inequalities listed in  Davis-Stober (2010) . Let

                               P 
                            
                         
                       is the set of all 0/1 vectors that satisfy inequalities  (2)–(8) .

In this section, I define the compatible simple lexicographic semiorder polytope and present a series of results on its facet structure.

The convex polytope

Inequalities  (2)–(5)  constitute  axiomatic  inequalities, in the sense of  Fiorini (2001) , while inequalities  (6)–(8)  are the  trivial  inequalities. The next three theorems show that

                       and that the axiomatic inequalities together with the trivial inequalities provide a complete list of facet-defining inequalities for any finite  
                         n 
                      .  
                         Theorem 1

Inequalities   (2)–(8)   provide a complete linear description of

The above proof provides an algorithm to decompose any element in  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       into a finite convex sum over elements from the set

                                        0

                         Theorem 2 
                         
                            
                               
                                  
                                     P

                            is a full dimensional polytope in

P 
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        CSLS 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     n 
                                  
                               
                             
                            is a full dimensional polytope in  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     R 
                                  
                                  
                                     n 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        n 
                                        − 
                                        1 
                                        ) 
                                     
                                  
                               
                            
                            .

Proof 
                         The result will follow if we construct a set of points (matrices), denoted  
                               
                                  
                                     X 
                                  
                                  
                                     ′ 
                                  
                               
                            , such that  
                               
                                  
                                     X 
                                  
                                  
                                     ′ 
                                  
                               
                               ⊆ 
                               
                                  
                                     X 
                                  
                                  
                                     P 
                                  
                               
                             and all points in  
                               
                                  
                                     X 
                                  
                                  
                                     ′ 
                                  
                               
                             are affinely independent in  
                               
                                  
                                     R 
                                  
                                  
                                     n 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        n 
                                        − 
                                        1 
                                        ) 
                                     
                                  
                               
                            . First, consider the set of all compatible simple lexicographic semiorders over  
                               n 
                             alternatives such that the binary relation  
                               
                                  
                                     S 
                                  
                                  
                                     2 
                                  
                               
                               = 
                               ∅ 
                            . In our matrix notation, this corresponds to the set,  
                               
                                  
                                     S 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                            , defined as follows,  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        
                                           S 
                                        
                                        
                                           ∗ 
                                        
                                     
                                     ≔ 
                                     
                                        { 
                                        
                                           x 
                                        
                                        ∈ 
                                        
                                           
                                              X 
                                           
                                           
                                              P 
                                           
                                        
                                        : 
                                        
                                           
                                              x 
                                           
                                           
                                              i 
                                              , 
                                              j 
                                           
                                        
                                        = 
                                        0 
                                        , 
                                        
                                        ∀ 
                                        
                                           ( 
                                           i 
                                           , 
                                           j 
                                           ) 
                                        
                                        ∈ 
                                        
                                           { 
                                           1 
                                           , 
                                           2 
                                           , 
                                           … 
                                           , 
                                           n 
                                           } 
                                        
                                        : 
                                        i 
                                        > 
                                        j 
                                        } 
                                     
                                     . 
                                  
                               
                             The set  
                               
                                  
                                     S 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                             is the set of all compatible simple lexicographic semiorder matrices such that all elements below the main diagonal are equal to zero. The set  
                               
                                  
                                     S 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                             corresponds exactly to the set of all semiorders over  
                               n 
                             alternatives compatible with a fixed linear ordering of  
                               A 
                             ( Davis-Stober, 2010; Mirkin, 1972; Sharp, 1972 ). Let  
                               
                                  
                                     X 
                                  
                                  
                                     1 
                                  
                                  
                                     ′ 
                                  
                               
                             be a set of  
                               
                                  
                                     n 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        n 
                                        − 
                                        1 
                                        ) 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     2 
                                  
                               
                             many elements from  
                               
                                  
                                     S 
                                  
                                  
                                     ∗ 
                                  
                               
                             such that all elements in  
                               
                                  
                                     X 
                                  
                                  
                                     1 
                                  
                                  
                                     ′

Theorem 3 
                         
                            Inequalities   (2)–(8)   are facet-defining for  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     P 
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        CSLS 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     n 
                                  
                               
                             
                            for any finite  
                            
                               n 
                            
                            .

Inequalities   (2)–(8)   are facet-defining for  
                            
                               
                                  
                                     P 
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        CSLS 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     n 
                                  
                               
                             
                            for any finite  
                            
                               n 
                            
                            .

Proof 
                         Since the inequalities from  (2)–(8)  provide a full linear description of  
                               
                                  
                                     P 
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        CSLS 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     n 
                                  
                               
                             ( Theorem 1 ) and the polytope  
                               
                                  
                                     P 
                                  
                                  
                                     
                                        CSLS 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     n 
                                  
                               
                             is full-dimensional ( Theorem 2 ), the above result follows if we show that no inequality from  (2)–(8)  is implied by any combination of the others. We proceed by selecting an arbitrary inequality from each class and constructing a point such that  only  the inequality of interest is violated. For all of the following cases, let  
                               k 
                             be a scalar such that  
                               0 
                               < 
                               k 
                               < 
                               1 
                            , and let  
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               ∈ 
                               
                                  
                                     R 
                                  
                                  
                                     n 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        n 
                                        − 
                                        1 
                                        ) 
                                     
                                  
                               
                             be arranged as a matrix with the same indexing as  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        x 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     P 
                                  
                               
                            , where  
                               
                                  
                                     
                                        x 
                                     
                                  
                                  
                                     P 
                                  
                               
                             is the characteristic vector of a simple lexicographic semiorder,  
                               P 
                            . 
                         
                            Case  1 (Inequality  (2) ). Let  
                               
                                  x 
                               
                               ∈ 
                               
                                  
                                     R 
                                  
                                  
                                     n 
                                     
                                        ( 
                                        n 
                                        − 
                                        1 
                                        ) 
                                     
                                  
                               
                             be defined such that  
                               
                                  
                                     x 
                                  
                                  
                                     i 
                                     , 
                                     j 
                                  
                               
                               = 
                               k 
                             (where  
                               i 
                               < 
                               j 
                            ) and all entries above and to the right of  
                               
                                  
                                     x 
                                  
                                  
                                     i 
                                     ,

Since the inequalities from  (2)–(8)  provide a full linear description of

We proceed by selecting an arbitrary inequality from each class and constructing a point such that  only  the inequality of interest is violated. For all of the following cases, let

                                              0 
                                           
                                           
                                              – 
                                           
                                        
                                     
                                     .

Clearly, inequality  (8)  is violated. It is routine to verify that all remaining inequalities are satisfied, thus completing the proof. □

                       presents an enumeration of the number of vertices and facets for

It is interesting that  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       admits a relatively simple facet structure.  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       is a 0/1 polytope, in that it can be described as the convex hull of a set of vertices with each element of each vertex equal to either 0 or 1. This family of polytopes is of great importance in combinatorial optimization (e.g.,  Nemhauser & Wolsey, 1988 ;  Ziegler, 1999 ) with some of the most notable members being the traveling salesman polytope ( Billera & Sarangarajan, 1996; Fischetti, 1991; Grötschel & Padberg, 1985 ), cut polytopes ( Deza & Laurent, 1997 ), weak order polytope ( Fiorini & Fishburn, 2004 ), bi-order polytope ( Christophe et al., 2004 ), approval-voting polytope ( Doignon & Fiorini, 2004; Doignon & Regenwetter, 1997 ), and the linear ordering polytope ( Block & Marschak, 1960; Grötschel, Jünger, & Reinelt, 1985; Suck, 1992 ). Many of the aforementioned 0/1 polytopes have exceedingly complex facet structures. For example, a complete facet description of the weak order polytope is only known for  
                         n 
                         ≤ 
                         5 
                      , and for the case  
                         n 
                         = 
                         5 
                       the weak order polytope has over 75,000 facets ( Fiorini, 2001; Regenwetter & Davis-Stober, in press )! Thus, the simplicity of the 0/1 polytope definition belies an underlying complexity. The polytope  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                      , by contrast, admits both a complete facet description for any  
                         n 
                       and relatively few facets when compared to other 0/1 polytopes such as the weak order polytope. In this way,  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       bears some similarity to the multiple choice polytope ( Falmagne, 1978; Fiorini, 2004 ).

Similar to the weak order polytope ( Fiorini & Fishburn, 2004; Regenwetter & Davis-Stober, in press ), linear order polytope (e.g.,  Block & Marschak, 1960 ), and multiple choice polytope ( Falmagne, 1978 ), we can interpret  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       as a decision making model of probabilistic choice. Note that the simple lexicographic semiorder definition allows the possibility that two choice alternatives are equivalent, i.e., a decision maker may be indifferent between two choice alternatives. As a result, we consider systems of ternary choice probabilities as opposed to systems of binary choice probabilities.

A decision maker satisfies  
                         
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               M 
                               X 
                            
                         
                       if, and only if, his/her ternary choice probabilities satisfy  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                      . Said another way, the  
                         
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               M 
                               X 
                            
                         
                       model states that the probability of a DM choosing choice alternative  
                         a 
                       over choice alternative  
                         b 
                       is the marginal probability of all compatible simple lexicographic semiorders in  
                         
                            
                               X 
                            
                            
                               P 
                            
                         
                       such that  
                         a 
                       is strictly preferred to  
                         b 
                      . In this way testing whether a decision maker’s ternary choice probabilities are consistent with being induced by simple lexicographic semiorders is equivalent to testing whether their ternary choice probabilities satisfy the facet-defining inequalities of  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                      . Evaluating the goodness-of-fit of these inequalities with choice data requires an application of order-compatible statistical inference, of which both classical ( Davis-Stober, 2009 ) and Bayesian ( Myung, Karabatsos, & Iverson, 2005 ) solutions are available.

As an illustration, I will consider data from two subjects from an unpublished study. These data were collected under a replication of the well-known  Tversky (1969)  intransitivity of preferences Experiment I. In the original experiment, Tversky repeatedly presented subjects with all pairwise comparisons of five carefully designed 2-branch lotteries. These lotteries were designed so that a subject would violate the transitivity of preference axiom should he or she utilize a simple lexicographic semiorder strategy when choosing among them. The gambles in our replication were identical to those in  Tversky (1969)  with the exception that monetary payoffs were increased according to 2009 dollar values. In our replication, each paired comparison was presented a total of thirty times with filler trials in between presentations to help counter memory effects. Our experimental paradigm also differed from  Tversky (1969)  in that we allowed subjects to express indifference between the choice alternatives, as opposed to the original two-alternative forced choice framework.

We will consider the  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       polytope defined by all simple lexicographic semiorders compatible with the linear order defined by the probability of winning attribute. This ordering corresponds to Tversky’s original hypothesis that DMs would consider the probability of winning attribute first and consider the payoff attribute only if the two probability of winning values did not sufficiently differ. The ternary choice proportions from Subject 1 are provided in  Appendix  and, after statistical analysis via the methodology in  Davis-Stober (2009) , are found to significantly reject this  
                         
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               M 
                               X 
                            
                         
                       mixture model with  
                         p 
                         < 
                         0.01 
                      . This result provides a strong rejection of this lexicographic semiorder structure for this subject, as this mixture model allows an arbitrary probability distribution over  any  set of simple lexicographic semiorders consistent with the probability of winning being considered first, followed by payoff. Therefore, we can strongly state that it is quite unlikely that this subject was following the simple lexicographic semiorder strategy Tversky theorized.

On the other hand, should a subject  not  significantly violate the  
                         
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               M 
                               X 
                            
                         
                       mixture model, then this too is informative. I will now consider another subject from the same unpublished study. Subject 2’s observed choice proportions (see  Appendix ) do not significantly violate  
                         
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               M 
                               X 
                            
                         
                       at  
                         α 
                         = 
                         0.05 
                      . This is, of course, an informative result only if the  
                         
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               M 
                               X 
                            
                         
                       mixture model makes restrictive predictions. To answer this, I have calculated, via Monte Carlo simulation, the proportion of all ternary choice probabilities that satisfy Inequalities  (2)–(8)  for up to five choice alternatives; see  Table 2 
                      . Note that these calculations hold for  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       for any fixed linear ordering of  
                         A 
                      .

It is remarkable that Subject 2’s observed choice proportions satisfy the  
                         
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               M 
                               X 
                            
                         
                       mixture model in this way, given the  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       polytope occupies less than one-fifth of one-percent of the space of all possible ternary choice probabilities for five choice alternatives. Thus, this subject’s data strongly support the notion that he or she is following a simple lexicographic semiorder strategy similar to what Tversky theorized. To summarize, the  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       polytope as a mixture model provides an informative set of hypotheses. Should choice data violate the model, we can reject the simple lexicographic semiorder structure completely as it allows an arbitrary probability distribution over all compatible simple lexicographic semiorders. On the other hand, should the data satisfy it then the model is well supported.

I have presented an analysis of the convex polytope,  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                      , defined as the convex hull of all compatible simple lexicographic semiorders. This polytope is a full dimensional 0/1 polytope in  
                         
                            
                               R 
                            
                            
                               n 
                               
                                  ( 
                                  n 
                                  − 
                                  1 
                                  ) 
                               
                            
                         
                      . I presented a list of facet-defining inequalities that are both necessary and sufficient for all  
                         n 
                      , where  
                         n 
                         = 
                         
                            | 
                            A 
                            | 
                         
                      . I enumerated the number of vertices and facets as a function of  
                         n 
                      . I demonstrated that this polytope is equivalent to a mixture model of ternary choice over the set of all simple lexicographic semiorders.

The polytope  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       is a 0/1 polytope in that it is described as the convex hull of a set of points with all entries equal to either 0 or 1. There exists an extensive literature on 0/1 polytopes highlighting their many interesting features (e.g.,  Ziegler, 1999 ).  Fleiner, Kaibel, and Rote (2000)  provide an upper bound on the number of facets that a 0/1 polytope may have as a function of dimension. The polytope  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       is an interesting contribution as its number of facets is in fact quite small compared to its number of vertices, yet  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       is not a  simple  0/1 polytope, in the sense of  Kaibel and Wolff (2000) , in which any simple 0/1 polytope is the Cartesian product of 0/1 simplices. It is also worth noting that the coefficients of the facet-defining inequalities of  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       are always either 0, 1 or −1 regardless of dimension, in contrast to other 0/1 polytopes whose facets may have very large coefficient values ( Alon & Vũ, 1997; Ziegler, 1999 ).

The simple lexicographic semiorder structure is derived under assumptions consistent with choice alternatives having two salient features that trade-off with one another, e.g., “risk versus reward” or “quality versus cost”. Future work could explore applications of the  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       polytope to preference and consumer choice data. An analysis of the  
                         
                            
                               P 
                            
                            
                               
                                  CSLS 
                               
                            
                            
                               n 
                            
                         
                       under the mixture model interpretation could reveal which “feature” a DM bases his or her decision upon. Future directions of this work could also explore generalizations of the lexicographic semiorder structure and their corresponding polytopes. It may be interesting to explore generalizations where the traces of the semiorders are not required to be the reverse of one another. It may be also be interesting to consider the lexicographic semiorder polytope induced by multiple semiorders instead of two.
